Different possibilities for the increased abundance of HGT on the outliers include, for example: (1) they may have specific sequence features that can serve to integrate HGT genes that are subsequently re-arranged and embedded in other locations in the genome; (2) it may reflect a lower density of essential gene functionalities in outliers, which could thus support a higher density of random insertions; or (3) there might be a lower level of recombination on these chromosomes, reducing the rate of removal of deleterious alleles via sexual recombination. None of these scenarios are mutually exclusive.</p><p>In the <i>Bathycoccus</i> genome, the gene copy number is highly expanded for four specific gene families, phenomena not found (or at very low copy number expansion) in other Mamiellales or other algae (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#Tab3">3</a>). Of these, two are involved in the metabolism of sialic acids, that is, sialyltransferases (69 gene copies) and sialidases (23 gene copies), the two others being ankyrin-repeat proteins (149 gene copies) and zinc finger proteins (48 gene copies) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#Tab3">3</a>). Among these 289 gene copies, 105 (36%) are represented within the 428 probable genes acquired by HGT, representing 24% of them.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Expanded gene families in the <i>Bathycoccus</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Sialic acid metabolism in <i>Bathycoccus</i></h3><p>The two enzyme families involved in the metabolism of sialic acids are not present in other known green algae genomes, and both gene families are dispersed all along the <i>Bathycoccus</i> genome without evident clustering or tandem duplication. Although, on average, 15% of the genes have introns in <i>Bathycoccus</i>, no introns (except three genes; Figure S11 in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM1">1</a>) were found in any gene from both families. Genes annotated as sialyltransferases correspond to glycosyltransferases family 29 in the CAZy classification, which comprises enzymes able to transfer sialic residues during glycosylation of proteins or lipids [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Cantarel BL, Coutinho PM, Rancurel C, Bernard T, Lombard V, Henrissat B: The Carbohydrate-Active EnZymes database (CAZy): an expert resource for Glycogenomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 2009, D233-238. 37 Database" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d25349e2954">42</a>]. All the <i>Bathycoccus</i> sialyltransferases showed a metazoan taxonic affiliation and none of them gave significant hits with bacteria. These enzymes are type II single pass membrane proteins usually known to be anchored in the Golgi membranes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Jeanneau C, Chazalet V, Aug C, Soumpasis DM, Harduin-Lepers A, Delannoy P, Imberty A, Breton C: Structure-function analysis of the human sialyltransferase ST3GalI. J Biol Chem. 2004, 279: 13461-13468. 10.1074/jbc.M311764200." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d25349e2961">43</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Harduin-Lepers A, Mollicone R, Delannoy P, Oriol R: The anormal sialyltransferase-related genes: a phylogenetic approach. Glycobiology. 2005, 15: 805-817. 10.1093/glycob/cwi063." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d25349e2964">44</a>]. A potential hydrophobic transmembrane domain was detected on the amino-terminal extremities of all the <i>Bathycoccus</i> sialyltransferases (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#Fig6">6a</a>). For almost all the 69 genes (only 19 are known in human), the sialyltransferase domain is located in the carboxy-terminal part of the protein, whereas the amino-terminal domain is composed of a highly variable stem region (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#Fig6">6a</a>). Although the existence of complete and active sialyltransferases in plants is still a matter of debate [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Harduin-Lepers A, Mollicone R, Delannoy P, Oriol R: The animal sialyltransferases and sialyltransferase-related genes: a phylogenetic approach. Glycobiology. 2005, 15: 805-817. 10.1093/glycob/cwi063. A published erratum appears in Glycobiology 2005, 15:21G" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d25349e2976">45</a>], all four metazoan consensus motifs were found in the <i>Bathycoccus</i> genes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fgb-2012-13-8-r74/MediaObjects/13059_2012_Article_2952_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fgb-2012-13-8-r74/MediaObjects/13059_2012_Article_2952_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>Sialyltransferase gene family and external scales covering</b> <i>Bathycoccus</i>. <b>(a)</b> Structural organization of the <i>Bathycoccus</i> RCC1105 sialyltransferase gene family. TM, transmembrane domain. L(1), S(2), (3) and VS(4) correspond to the four metazoan consensus motifs described for this gene family [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Jeanneau C, Chazalet V, Aug C, Soumpasis DM, Harduin-Lepers A, Delannoy P, Imberty A, Breton C: Structure-function analysis of the human sialyltransferase ST3GalI. J Biol Chem. 2004, 279: 13461-13468. 10.1074/jbc.M311764200." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3004">43</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Harduin-Lepers A, Mollicone R, Delannoy P, Oriol R: The anormal sialyltransferase-related genes: a phylogenetic approach. Glycobiology. 2005, 15: 805-817. 10.1093/glycob/cwi063." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3007">44</a>]. Letters in the scheme below are the amino acid one-letter code with alternative possibilities for positions indicated between brackets. <b>(b-d)</b> Details of external scales covering <i>Bathycoccus</i> RCC1105 cells; white arrows indicate positions of external scales around the plasmic membrane while the black arrow indicates one intracellular scale inside a vesicle.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The second gene family includes sialidases (or neuraminidases), which are enzymes cleaving the terminal sialic acid residues from glycoproteins or glycolipids. Again, this gene expansion is specific to <i>Bathycoccus</i>. In contrast to the previous family, no clear domain organization could be defined in sialidases, but some key amino acids known to be involved in the catalytic activity are conserved in the <i>Bathycoccus</i> family. The taxonomic origin of the sialidases is less clear than that for the sialyltransferases discussed above, and could correspond to either metazoans or bacteria. For sialidases, scores are globally weak and best blast hits are found mostly with hypothetical proteins either from the choanoflagellate <i>Monosiga brevicolis</i> or from the green alga <i>Chlorella variabilis</i> (where only one sialidase has been annotated).</p><p>The expansion of these two enzyme families prompted us to look for specific potential 'sialic acid' metabolism in <i>Bathycoccus</i>. The composition of flagellar scales in <i>Scherffelia dubia</i> (phylum Chlorophyta, class Chlorodendrophyceae) was found to be a mix of acidic polysaccharides having similar structures to sialic acids [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Melkonian M, Preisig HR: A light and electron microscopic study of Scherffelia dubia, a new member of the scaly green flagellates (Prasinophyceae). Nord J Bot. 1986, 6: 235-256. 10.1111/j.1756-1051.1986.tb00876.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3049">46</a>]. Although the chemical nature of the scales covering the <i>Bathycoccus</i> cell membrane is unknown, it is tempting to establish a correlation between the potential biosynthetic pathway of these scales and the expansion of gene families involved in the metabolism of sialic acids. Furthermore, we confirmed previous electron microscopy studies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Eikrem W, Throndsen J: The ultrastructure of Bathycoccus gen nov and B. prasinos sp Nov, a non-motile picoplanktonic alga (Chlorophyta, Prasinophyceae) from the Mediterranean and Atlantic. Phycologia. 1990, 29: 344-350. 10.2216/i0031-8884-29-3-344.1." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3055">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Moestrup O, Walne PL: Studies on scale morphogenesis in the Golgi apparatus of Pyramimonas tetrarhynchus (Prasinophyceae). J Cell Sci. 1979, 36: 437-459." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3059">47</a>] showing that, in <i>Bathycoccus</i> as in other Mamiellophyceae, scale biosynthesis occurs inside intracellular vesicles with striking resemblance to Golgi vesicles (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#Fig6">6b-d</a>); that is, in agreement with the notion that they might be produced by sialyltransferases located at the luminal side of intracellular vesicles. Scales almost identical to those of <i>B. prasinos</i> are observed in the more closely related <i>Mantoniella squamata</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Moestrup O: Scale structure in Mantoniella squamata, with some comments on the phylogeny of the Prasinophyceae (Chlorophyta). Phycologia. 1990, 29: 437-442. 10.2216/i0031-8884-29-4-437.1." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3074">48</a>], where they are also extruded to the surface after transport via the Golgi body [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Melkonian M, Becker B, Becker D: Scale formation in algae. J Electron Microscopy Technique. 1991, 17: 165-178. 10.1002/jemt.1060170205." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3078">49</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Becker B: Anterograde transport of algal scales through the Golgi complex is not mediated by vesicles. Trends Cell Biol. 1995, 5: 305-307. 10.1016/S0962-8924(00)89047-9." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3081">51</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Other <i>Bathycoccus</i> expanded gene families</h3><p>One of the two other highly expanded gene families in the <i>Bathycoccus</i> genome are ankyrin-repeat proteins (149 gene copies). This family is also expanded, although to a lesser extent, in the <i>Micromonas</i> strain CCMP1545 (56 copies), whereas only very few copies were detected in other Mamiellales (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#Tab3">3</a>). These genes have ankyrin repeats located in the carboxy-terminal part of the protein whereas the amino-terminal part has no hit in GenBank. There are also many other ankyrin repeats containing genes in <i>Bathycoccus</i> as in both plants and microalgae, but associated with different protein domains that often have predicted functionalities. Indeed, the ankyrin repeat is considered as one of the most common protein-protein interaction motifs in nature [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Al-Khodor S, Price CT, Kalia A, Kwaik A: Functional diversity of ankyrin repeats in microbial proteins. Trends Microbiol. 2010, 18: 132-139. 10.1016/j.tim.2009.11.004." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3108">52</a>]. The 149 <i>Bathycoccus</i>-specific genes were not distributed randomly among chromosomes, with the bigger chromosomes having few copies, whereas chromosomes 12 or 19 bear many tandem duplicated genes (Figure S12 in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM1">1</a>). No obvious function can be attributed to these genes. However, by analogy with the human membrane-associated ankyrin, which is responsible for the attachment of the cytoskeleton to the plasma membrane, it is possible that a number of these genes might function in some way to bind extracellular scales to the plasmic membrane, although experimental evidence is lacking. It has been shown, however, by electron microscopy coupled to immunogold that scales in <i>Scherffelia dubia</i> are linked to the membrane by glycoproteins [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Melkonian M, Preisig HR: A light and electron microscopic study of Scherffelia dubia, a new member of the scaly green flagellates (Prasinophyceae). Nord J Bot. 1986, 6: 235-256. 10.1111/j.1756-1051.1986.tb00876.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3121">46</a>]. In addition, in <i>Tetraselmis striata</i> (Chlorodendrophyceae) some scale-associated glycoproteins may provide connections between scales and the underlying flagellar membrane [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Becker D, Melkonian M: N-linked glycoproteins associated with flagellar scales in a flagellate green alga: characterization of interactions. Eur J Cell Biol. 1992, 57: 109-116." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3127">50</a>].</p><p>The last group of expanded genes in <i>Bathycoccus</i> are zinc finger proteins. There are many zinc finger proteins in microalgae and in plants, but the family specifically expanded in <i>Bathycoccus</i> is most related to the C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub>-type zinc finger DNA-binding domain of certain integrases, which share a common alpha/beta two-layer sandwich core structure. The typical organization of the 48 copies identified in the <i>Bathycoccus</i> genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#Tab3">3</a>) includes a short amino-terminal part (around 20 to 40 amino acids) followed by a strongly acidic region (10 to 20 amino acids) and by 2 to 6 C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub> domains. Zinc finger proteins were originally identified as DNA-binding domains, although a growing body of evidence suggests an important and widespread role for these domains in protein binding. There are even examples of zinc fingers that support both DNA and protein interactions, and, globally, C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub> protein-protein interactions are proving to be more abundant than previously appreciated [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Brayer KJ, Segal DJ: Keep your fingers off my DNA: protein-protein interactions mediated by C2H2 zinc finger domains. Cell Biochem Biophys. 2008, 50: 111-131. 10.1007/s12013-008-9008-5." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3158">53</a>].</p><p>The most parsimonious explanation for the abundance of the four expanded gene families would be an initial single HGT event followed by expansion in the <i>Bathycoccus</i> genome. The potential function of these four gene families and their expansion only in <i>Bathycoccus</i> also suggest that they could all be involved in the biosynthesis, exportation and fixation of the scales around the external membrane, and possibly for protection of the cell. Several other members of the Mamiellales have morphologically similar scales around the cells, but they are absent in the two genera <i>Micromonas</i> and <i>Ostreococcus</i>. The most parsimonious evolutionary scenario to explain these observations is that the scale synthesis pathway was acquired by the ancestor of the Mamiellales (or even before) and has been lost in the two naked genera. This scenario predicts that similar gene family expansions should be found in the genomes of other scaled Mamiellaophyceae but not in <i>Micromonas</i> and <i>Ostreococcus</i>. This is the case for <i>Micromonas</i> and <i>Ostreococcus</i>, but the genome sequences of other scaled species are not yet available.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>Mamiellophyceae, and more particularly the three genera <i>Bathycoccus</i>, <i>Micromonas</i> and <i>Ostreococcus</i>, are dominant in different marine areas, where they can play an important role in the primary biomass production. However, the ecological importance of <i>Bathycoccus</i> has probably been overlooked these past years, although it was sporadically mentioned in several studies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Knight-Jones EW, Walne PR: Chromulina pusilla Butcher; a dominant member of the ultraplankton. Nature. 1951, 167: 445-10.1038/167445a0." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3210">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Marie D, Zhu F, Balagu V, Ras J, Vaulot D: Eukaryotic picoplankton communities of the Mediterranean Sea in summer assessed by molecular approaches (DGGE, TTGE, QPCR). FEMS Microbiol Ecol. 2006, 55: 403-415. 10.1111/j.1574-6941.2005.00058.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3214">16</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Treusch AH, Demir-Hilton E, Vergin KL, Worden AZ, Carlson CA, Donatz MG, Burton RM, Giovannoni SJ: Phytoplankton distribution patterns in the northwestern Sargasso Sea revealed by small subunit rRNA genes from plastids. ISME J. 2011, 6: 481-492." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3217">18</a>]. The availability of this genome, coupled to the development of new sequencing possibilities for metagenomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Cheung MK, Au CH, Chu KH, Kwan HS, Wong CK: Composition and genetic diversity of picoeukaryotes in subtropical coastal waters as revealed by 454 pyrosequencing. ISME J. 2010, 4: 1053-1059. 10.1038/ismej.2010.26." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3220">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Marie D, Shi X L, Rigaut-Jalabert F, Vaulot D: Use of flow cytometric sorting to better assess the diversity of small photosynthetic eukaryotes in the English Channel. FEMS Microbiol Ecol. 2010, 72: 165-178. 10.1111/j.1574-6941.2010.00842.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3223">20</a>] from various marine environments, opens the door to future comparative studies and to a better understanding of the adaptations of the organisms to their environment.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13"><i>B. prasinos</i> RCC1105 genome and EST sequencing and annotation</h3><p>The sequenced strain <i>B. prasinos</i> RCC1105 was isolated in the bay of Banyuls sur mer at the SOLA station (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM1">1</a>). The genomic DNA was extracted from cell pellets containing a collective total of 6.4  10<sup>10</sup> cells, using a cetyl trimethyl ammonium bromide protocol (adapted from [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Winnepenninckx B, Backeljau T, De Wachter R: Extraction of high molecular weight DNA from molluscs. Trends Genet. 1993, 9: 407-" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3249">54</a>]). The <i>Bathycoccus</i> genome was sequenced using Sanger technology on three independent shot-gun libraries with insert sizes of 3 (TK0AAA, vector pcdna2.1 (BstXI)), 10 (TK0AAB, vector pCNS (BstXI)) and 50 kb (TK0ACA, vector pBeloBAC11 (HindIII) and TK0ACB, vector pBeloBAC11 (BamHI)), resulting in 230,496 reads (180 Mb), 118,070 reads (152 Mb) and 10,368 reads (14 Mb), respectively. After trimming, read numbers were 223,577 reads (174 Mb) for the 3 kb library, 112,842 reads (145 Mb) for the 10 kb library and 8,189 reads (11 Mb) for the 50 kb library, and represented a coverage of 22-fold from 330 Mb of sequenced DNA. The data were assembled using the Genoscope pipe-line that includes the software Arachne 3.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Batzoglou S, Jaffe DB, Stanley K, Butler J, Gnerre S, Mauceli E, Berger B, Mesirov JP, Lander ES: ARACHNE: a whole-genome shotgun assembler. Genome Res. 2002, 12: 177-189.52. 10.1101/gr.208902." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3256">55</a>]. ESTs were sequenced from a <i>Bathycoccus</i> culture grown to log phase (10<sup>7</sup> cells/ml), harvested by centrifugation and the cell pellets were immediately flash frozen in liquid nitrogen. The total RNA was extracted using the TriReagent (Sigma-Aldrich, Saint-Quentin, France) protocol and mRNAs purified using Poly(A)Purist (Ambion-Applied Bioystems, Saint Aubin, France). Complementary DNAs were constructed and cloned using the CloneMiner procedure (InvitroGen, Saint Aubin, France) with some minor modifications. EST sequences were obtained using pyrosequencing technology developed by Roche (Boulogne-Billancourt, France). A total of 253,791 EST reads were processed through the Genoscope EST pipeline. Short (&lt;60 bp) and low complexity sequences were identified and removed. Clustering and assembly of all 251,875 filtered EST reads resulted in 8,370 EST consensus sequences.</p><p>The genome was annotated using the EuGene [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Schiex T, Moisan A, Rouz P: EUGNE: an eukaryotic gene finder that combines several sources of evidence. Lect Notes Comput Sci. 2001, 2066: 111-125. 10.1007/3-540-45727-5_10." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3267">56</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Foissac S, Gouzy J, Rombauts S, Math C, Amselem J, Sterck L, Van de Peer Y, Rouz P, Schiex T: Genome annotation in plants and fungi: EuGne as a model platform. Curr Bioinformatics. 2008, 3: 87-97. 10.2174/157489308784340702." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3270">57</a>] gene finding system with Splice- Machine [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Degroeve S, Saeys Y, De Baets B, Rouz P, Van de Peer Y: SpliceMachine: predicting splice sites from high-dimensional local context representations. Bioinformatics. 2005, 21: 1332-1338. 10.1093/bioinformatics/bti166." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3273">58</a>] signal sensor components trained specifically on <i>Bathycoccus</i> datasets. The functional annotation resulted from the synthesis of InterPro and the BLASTP hits against the non-redundant UniProt database. Gene Ontology assignments were derived from the InterPro results. Gene Ontology enrichment analysis was performed using the hypergeometric distribution with Bonferonni correction for multiple hypothesis testing and corrected <i>P</i>-values &lt;0.05 were retained as significant. The resulting database is publicly available at [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Genome Sequences and Annotations at Ghent University. [&#xA;                    http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/genomes&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3283">59</a>] in a format that includes browse and query options and the genome has been submitted to GenBank.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Comparative sequence and expression analysis</h3><p>Starting from all protein-coding genes from the included species (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#Tab1">1</a>), only retaining the longest transcript if alternative splicing variants exist, protein sequences were used to construct gene families by applying sequence-based protein clustering. First, an all against all sequence comparison was performed using BLASTP, applying an E-value threshold of 1e-05 and retaining the best 500 hits [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Altschul SF, Madden TL, Schffer AA, Zhang J, Zhang Z, Miller W, Lipman DJ: Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 1997, 25: 3389-33402. 10.1093/nar/25.17.3389." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3297">60</a>]. Next, the complete sequence similarity graph was processed using Tribe-MCL (mclblastline, default parameters except I = 2 and scheme = 4) to identify gene families. A set of 154 single-copy core gene families was used to construct the phylogenetic tree depicted in Figure S1 in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM1">1</a> (see also Additional files <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM2">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM3">3</a>).</p><p>The boundaries of all Mamiellales BOC1 regions were manually delineated based on gene coordinates, gene family information and GC content (Table S2 in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM1">1</a>). For non-Mamiellales, a 'virtual' BOC1 region was created by taking the best BLASTP hit for each <i>B. prasinos</i> RCC1105 BOC1 gene. Putative BOC1 Mamiellales core gene families (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#Fig6">6</a>, blue lines) were identified by first retaining only those families that contain at least one protein for each Mamiellales species. Next, each family was aligned and manually curated. This was done by inspecting and correcting, if necessary, the structural and functional annotation (NCBI BLAST results plus InterProScan) of all cluster members. For <i>Ostreococcus</i> sp. RCC809 no SOC could be identified in the current draft genome assembly (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Comparative genomics</h3><p>To detect co-linearity within and between species, i-ADHoRe 3.0 was used (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM1">1</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Proost S, Fostier J, De Witte D, Dhoedt B, Demeester P, Van de Peer Y, Vandepoele K: i-ADHoRe 3 0 - fast and sensitive detection of genomic homology in extremely large data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012, 40: e11-10.1093/nar/gkr955." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3338">61</a>] and all chromosomes from all species were compared against each other and significant colinear regions were identified. All gene colinearity can be browsed using the pico-PLAZA comparative genomics platform [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="pico-PLAZA: an integrative resource for cross-species genome analysis in algae. [&#xA;                    http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/pico-plaza/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3341">62</a>]. i-ADHoRe was run with the following settings: alignment_method gg, gap_size 30, cluster_gap 35, q_value 0.9, prob_cutoff 0.0001, anchor_points 5 and level_2_only false.</p><p>EST databases were retrieved from their respective public repositories and mapped on the Mamiellales genomes using GenomeThreader [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Gremme G, Brendel V, Sparks ME, Kurtz S: Engineering a software tool for gene structure prediction in higher organisms. Information Software Technol. 2005, 47: 965-978. 10.1016/j.infsof.2005.09.005." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3347">63</a>] with a minimum alignment score threshold of 0.95 and minimum transcript coverage of 0.89. Only uniquely mapped ESTs were retained and assigned to genes. When an EST with no strand information overlapped with two adjacent genes, it was assigned to the gene with the highest overlap. For the BOC expression analysis global gene, EST counts were first summarized per functional category. In a second stage, expression enrichment was determined by comparing for each functional category the fraction of BOC expressed genes against the overall fraction of BOC expressed genes (denoted 'relative BOC expression enrichment' in Figure S8 in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Analysis of potential horizontal gene transfer</h3><p>For each protein-coding gene a BLAST sequence similarity search was performed against the NCBI protein database, which contains the proteins of all sequenced <i>Ostreococcus</i>, <i>Micromonas</i> and <i>Chlamydomonas</i> species (E-value &lt;1e-05). Starting from a selection of BLAST hits a phylogenetic approach was used to identify the putative origin of all genes. Briefly, good hits (20% top hits relative to the best Bit score excluding query self-hits) were retained per gene, protein sequences and detailed taxonomic information was retrieved, a multiple sequence alignment was generated using MUSCLE and a maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree was constructed using PhyML (100 bootstrap sets, WAG model, kappa estimated, 4 substitution rate categories, gamma distribution parameter estimated, BIONJ starting tree, no topology, branch lengths and rate parameter optimization). For each query gene the corresponding tree topology was investigated to identify the nearest neighbor gene/clade, including bootstrap support, and determine the nearest neighbor taxonomic information. Genes showing complex punctuate patterns [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Rogers MB, Watkins RF, Harper JT, Durnford DG, Gray MW, Keeling PJ: A complex and punctate distribution of three eukaryotic genes derived by lateral gene transfer. BMC Evol Biol. 2007, 7: 89-10.1186/1471-2148-7-89." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3370">64</a>] (that is, clustering with homologs from different phyla outside the Viridiplantae; labeled 'multi-kingdom' in Table S5 in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM1">1</a>) were excluded. Singletons refer to genes for which no phylogenetic analysis could be done because they only have a single BLAST hit based on the 20% top hits. Nearest neighbors with bootstrap support &gt;90% and gene coverage of 50% or more in the multiple alignment were scored as reliable HGT genes to estimate the fraction of eukaryotic origin. Although the low number of HGT genes found in <i>Arabidopsis</i> does not serve as a perfect negative control for the detection of HGT in unicellular green algae, it suggests that, when applied to a full set of proteins of a specific organism, this approach gives a conservative estimate of putative transfer events with a low number of false positives. To verify if, for some HGT genes, homologous genes exist in other algae that were missed during the process of gene annotation, a systematic sequence similarity search (using tblastn, E-value threshold 1e<sup>-05</sup> against intergenic sequences of <i>O. tauri</i>, <i>O. lucimarinus</i>, <i>Ostreococcus</i> RCC809, <i>M. pusilla</i> and <i>C. reinhardtii</i>) revealed that, on average, no homologous locus could be found for 93% of the HGT genes. A list of all HGT genes together with protein alignment and phylogenetic tree statistics is available in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#MOESM5">5</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">C-hunter analysis</h3><p>Four functional categories (two types with two subdivisions each) were defined and genes were assigned to each class, if applicable. The first type of functional category describes the expression state of a gene (based on uniquely mapped ESTs; is a gene expressed (number of ESTs &gt;0) or highly expressed (number of ESTs &gt;2)) while the second type describes the intron content of a gene (contains an intron (number of introns &gt;0) or contains a 'lot' of introns (number of introns &gt;2)). C-hunter [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Yi G, Sze SH, Thon MR: Identifying clusters of functionally related genes in genomes. Bioinformatics. 2007, 23: 1053-1060. 10.1093/bioinformatics/btl673." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3410">65</a>] software was used to identify, in all genomes, significant clusters of genes belonging to one of the four functional categories. The C-hunter thresholds for each category subdivision were determined by reviewing the average expression and intron content of all Mamiellales genes. C-hunter was run with the following parameters: &lt;C-hunter categories.go genome.index genome.go 2 80 80 0.001 50 T chunter output&gt;.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Accession numbers</h3><p>Sequence data from this article (the genome of <i>B. prasinos</i> RCC1105) can be found in the EMBL/GenBank data libraries under accession number [FO082258] (mitochondrion), [FO082259] (chloroplast), [FO082278] (chromosome 1), [FO082277] (chromosome 2), [FO082276] (chromosome 3), [FO082275] (chromosome 4), [FO082274] (chromosome 5), [FO082273] (chromosome 6), [FO082272] (chromosome 7), [FO082271] (chromosome 8), [FO082270] (chromosome 9), [FO082269] (chromosome 10), [FO082268] (chromosome 11), [FO082267] (chromosome 12), [FO082266] (chromosome 13), [FO082265] (chromosome 14), [FO082264] (chromosome 15), [FO082263] (chromosome 16), [FO082262] (chromosome 17), [FO082261] (chromosome 18), [FO082260] (chromosome19). The annotation of the genome can be found at the BOGAS web site [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Bathycoccus Genome Annotation Database at Ghent University. [&#xA;                    http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/webtools/bogas/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d25349e3424">66</a>]. EST data are available at the ENA database (accession number ERA148021) and raw genome sequencing data are available at the Trace archive database of the NCBI under the query: species_code="BATHYCOCCUS SP. BAN7".</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 7 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=A%20new%20marine%20picoeukaryote%20Ostreococcus%20tauri%20gen%20et%20sp.%20nov%20%28Chlorophyta%2C%20Prasinophyceae%29&amp;journal=Phycologia&amp;volume=4&amp;pages=285-292&amp;publication_year=1995&amp;author=Chr%C3%A9tiennot-Dinet%2CMJ&amp;author=Courties%2CC&amp;author=Vaquer%2CA&amp;author=Neveux%2CJ&amp;author=Claustre%2CH&amp;author=Lautier%2CJ&amp;author=Machado%2CMC">

Sequencing of the <i>Bathycoccus</i> genome has been supported by the Genoscope (Evry, France) and this research was supported by the CNRS and the University Pierre et Marie Curie (UMR 7232) and the Agence Nationale de la Recherche grants PHYTADAPT n NT09_567009 and Tara-Girus 09-PCS-GENM-218. We thank Igor Grigoriev, Brian Palenik and the Joint Genome Institute for the access to the <i>Ostreococcus</i> strain RCC809 genome. BV, KV and YVdP acknowledge the support of Ghent University (Multidisciplinary Research Partnership 'Bioinformatics: from nucleotides to networks') and the Interuniversity Attraction Poles Programme (IUAP P6/25), initiated by the Belgian State, Science Policy Office (BioMaGNet).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="author-information" data-title="Author information"><div                 <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-8-r74/email/correspondent/c1/new">Herv Moreau</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="additional-information" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="additional-information-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="additional-information">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="additional-information-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3><p>The authors declare that they have no competing interests.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Authors' contributions</h3><p>All authors have read and approved the manuscript for publication. ED, H-MMF, JP, MK, CDS, and AC performed research and sequencing. HM, BV, SR, MVB, GP, PR, and KV analyzed the data and wrote the paper. NG, PW, and YVdP wrote the paper. HM and KV designed the research.</p><p>Patrick Wincker, Yves Van de Peer contributed equally to this work.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec19" data-title="Electronic supplementary material"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec19-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec19">Electronic supplementary material</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec19-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div>
